Jamison Brereton

65
Agni
Par─Б┼Ыara ┼Ъ─Бktya
10 verses: dvipad─Б vir─Бj
As is generally the case with dvipad─Б vir─Бj meter, the hymn falls into two-verse units, essentially equivalent to a single trimeter verse (forty syllables, versus forty-four [triс╣гс╣нubh] or forty-eight [jagat─л]). The five-syllable units in which the verses are orga nized lend themselves to brief, discrete semantic and syntactic units, producing a mosaic-like effect. In this hymn, as in the following one, the mosaic fragments are often similesтАФespecially pronounced here in the middle verses 5тАУ6, though found throughout. Each simile highlights a particular aspect of Agni.
The hymn also follows a ritual and mythological trajectory. It begins with the hidden Agni (vs. 1), alluding in mythological terms to the well-known story of AgniтАЩs flight and rediscovery by the gods, which is treated in verses 2тАУ3, but also, in ritual terms, to the fire hidden in the wood before kindling. This kindling is accom
plished by the end of the hymn, where Agni spreads through the firewood and awakens at dawn, conspicuous and visible from afar (vss. 7тАУ10). The relationship of Agni to waters, in his identity as Ap─Бm Nap─Бt, is another theme in the hymn (vss. 4тАУ7, 9), though the epithet Ap─Бm Nap─Бt is not mentioned.

Wilson

English translation:

тАЬ[1-2] The firm and plural cid divinites followed you, Agni, by your footmarks, when hiding in the hollow (of the water), like a thief (who has stolen) an animal; you, claiming oblations, and bearing them to the gods; all the deities who are entitled to worship, sit down near to you.тАЭ

Jamison Brereton

(You) did the clever, of one accord, follow along your tracks;
all those worthy of the sacrifice reverently approached you.

Jamison Brereton Notes

something else is enclosing Agni, configured as a calf (v├бtsaс╣Г), using p├бri тИЪas.

(I wonder if the somewhat pleonastic+++(=redundant)+++ presence of bh├║vat in our passage is meant to resonate with those p├бri тИЪbh┼л passages.) Thus, though in our passage Agni is clearly the object of enclosure in the myth of his hiding in the waters, he himself can take the role of encloser in this tight-knit collection - allowing for the possibility that he might here be supplied as subjective genitive with p├бriс╣гс╣нiс╕е, save for the mythological context.

All in all, I.65.3cd packs a lot into its ten syllables and five words, as witnessed by the many more words (more than ten times as many) in this entry required to describe its syntactic and semantic tricks.

Sayana bhashya empty

Wilson

English translation:

тАЬ[3-4] The gods followed the traces of the fugitive; the search spread everywhere, and earth became like heaven; the waters swelled (to conceal him), who was much enlarged by praise, and was manifested as it were in the womb in the waters, the source of sacrificial food.тАЭ

Commentary by S─Бyaс╣Зa: с╣Ъgveda-bh─Бс╣гya

So nileyata, so ─Бpaс╕е praviс╣гat, tam devaс╕е praiс╣гam aicchan; tam matsy─Бс╕е prabravit: he, concealing himself, entered into the waters; the gods wished for a messenger to him; a fish discovered him. A fish revealed to the gods where Agni had hidden (Taittiriya)

Jamison Brereton

The gods followed the commandments of truth.
Like heaven (enclosing) the earth was his enclosing.

Jamison Brereton Notes

The second half of this verse is very economically and ingeniously expressed.

First, the syntactic modes of simile and frame are entirely different. The frame consists of a single noun, nominalized from an underlying verb (p├бriс╣гс╣нiс╕е тАШenclosingтАЩ тЖР p├бri тИЪas тАШencloseтАЩ), with the object (Agni, an underlying objective genitive) to be supplied from context (тАЬthe enclosing [of Agni]тАЭ). The simile contains both subject and object in the appropriate cases (nom. dya├║с╕е and [presumably] acc. bh┼л╠Бma) but no verb, which must be generated from the noun p├бriс╣гс╣нiс╕е (тАЬas heaven [encloses] the earthтАЭ), which in turn was generated from the underlying verb. Thus, the frame and the simile are in syntactic complementary distribution - the former with a verb (or verb substitute) but no overt nominal complements, the latter with nominal complements but no overt verb. That the verb-y half is expressed nominally and the noun-y half as if it contained a governing verb is a further bit of cleverness. This syntactic skew produces case mismatch, with the nom. тАЬheavenтАЭ of the simile apparently compared to nom. тАЬthe enclosingтАЭ (p├бriс╣гс╣нir dya├║r n├б), and, further, the mismatch between the single (presumed) complement of the frame and the two overt complements in the simile gives an impression almost of case disharmony of the type treated in Jamison 1982, although in fact the objective gen. тАЬof AgniтАЭ that must be supplied matches the acc. object bh┼л╠Бma in the simile.

Or at least it matches bh┼л╠Бma acdg. to the standard renderings (Geldner, Renou, Witzel Got┼Н, L├╝ders, Varuс╣Зa p. 590, and the published translation). However, bh┼л╠Бma is a neuter and ambiguous as to case, and heaven enclosing the earth does not strike me as a standard Vedic image тАФ though it slips easily into our round-earth cosmological worldview. Far more commonly in Vedic heaven and earth together encompass something else (e.g., X.27.7, under an epithet) or fail to (II.16.3, again under an epithet), or they themselves are encompassed (see the expressions quoted in Jamison 1998). For a nicely balanced passage in which the two appear both as object and subject, see nearby (but attributed to a different poet) I.61.8cd p├бri dy─Б╠Бv─Бprthiv─л╠Бjabhra urv─л╠Б, n─Б╠Бsya t├й mahim─Б╠Бnam p├бri с╣гс╣нaс╕е тАЬHe held encircled broad heaven and earth; they did not encompass his greatness.тАЭ I therefore now wonder if bh┼л╠Бma should be taken as a nominative parallel to dya├║с╕е, with an object to be supplied (тАЬтАж as heaven [and] earth [encompass X]тАЭ) or even if dya├║с╕е тАж bh┼л╠Бma should be recombined into a dual dvandva (cf. dy─Б╠Бv─Б-bh┼лm─л╠Б, with slightly different 2nd member) and taken as an object (тАЬas [X encompasses] heaven [and] earthтАЭ). (Note that the disyllabic reading of di ya├║с╕е could accommodate a dy─Б╠Бv─Б.) In any case, I believe that the poet kept these possibilities open by the ambiguous concision of his wording.

Yet another aspect of this little expression is its relation to other such expressions in this small group of hymns attributed to Par─Б┼Ыara ┼Ъ─Бktya (I.65-73): twice elsewhere in this collection Agni is depicted as encompassing something else, with the lexemes p├бri тИЪbh┼л (I.68.2 p├бri тАж bh├║vat, I.69.2 p├бri тАж babh┼лtha), while in

Jamison Brereton Notes

These verses contain 3rd pl. act. root aorists ├бnu gman (2b) and ├бnu тАж guс╕е (3b) to the associated roots тИЪgam and тИЪg─Б respectively, with no apparent difference in semantics. Here one might almost invoke the much over-used explanation of metrical convenience: both of these 3rd plurals are always (gman) or almost always (guс╕е) p─Бda-final, with the first тАЬmaking positionтАЭ with a preceding light syllable (often ├бnu) and the latter not.

Like a steed urged to run in swift career, rushing like Sindhu, who may check his course?

Jamison Brereton

With wonder the waters strengthen the lovely child,
well-begotten in the lap of truth, in its womb.

Jamison Brereton Notes

The initial sequence in this vs. v├бrdhant─лm can, uncontroversially, consist of the 3rd pl. act. pres. v├бrdhanti and the enclitic pronoun ─лm, as the Pp. and all subsequent analyses take it. I wonder, however, if it should not be analyzed like nearby I.67.4 vid├бnt─лm (q.v.; also a Par─Б┼Ыara hymn), where I segment the sequence as vid├бnt ─лm, with the old secondary ending *-ant preserved because it was misanalyzed as -├бnti + ─лm. In I.67.4 there is stronger motivation to accept my reanalysis because vid├б- is otherwise only an aorist stem and should not have indicative forms with primary endings. Here, of course, v├бrdha- is a present stem and v├бrdhanti would be perfectly at home. Nonetheless, the first four vss. of this hymn treat the disappearance of Agni and his concealment in the waters and are couched in the past

– note esp. ├бnu guс╕е тАШthey followedтАЩ in 3a, the vs. paired with this one. I would therefore tentatively emend the published translation to тАЬthe waters strengthened the lovely child.тАЭ For this and other such passages (esp. I.65.4 and I.85.11), see my тАЬHidden in Plain Sight: Some Older Verb Endings in the Rig VedaтАЭ in a forthcoming festschrift.

It is unclear with what part of the sentence the instr. panv─Б╠Б should be construed. I take it with the verb v├бrdhant(i) (though I do not go as far as Oldenberg, who suggests that the phrase is equivalent to pan├бyanti), while Geldner (/Witzel Got┼Н) and Renou, in slightly different ways, take it with s├║┼Ыi┼Ыvim. The stem is a hapax and there is no obvious way to decide the matter, though the passages Renou adduces for his instr. of characterization (IX.85.11, 86.31 ┼Ы├н┼Ыum .. p├бnipnatam and III.1.13 ap─Б╠Бс╣Г g├бrbham тАж p├бniс╣гс╣нhaс╣Г j─Бt├бm) may tip the scales in his direction. So I might modify the published translation to тАЬThe waters strengthen(ed) the lovely child in his wonderтАЭ or (see below) тАЬтАж strengthen(ed) the one growing well with/in wonder,тАЭ though construing it with v├бrdhant(i) still seems to yield more sense.

The matter is made more difficult by the fact that s├║┼Ыi┼Ыvi- is also a hapax. It is generally taken as an adj. тАЬsch├╢n wachsendтАЭ (ultimately to тИЪ┼Ы┼л тАШswellтАЩ), and this is certainly possible. But because of ┼Ы├н┼Ыv─Б, instr. of ┼Ы├н┼Ыu- тАШchildтАЩ, in 10c, I follow Oldenberg (SBE, тАЬthe fine childтАЭ) and Renou (тАЬle beau Nouveau-n├йтАЭ) in taking it as a noun.

Griffith

Kin as a brother to his sister floods, he eats the woods as a King eats the rich.
When through the forest, urged by wind, he spreads, verily Agni shears the hair of earth.

Oldenberg

In the lap, in the womb of Rita, the waters nourish the fine child with praise, him who is well born.

Geldner

ihn ziehen die Wasser gro├Я, der unter Beifall sich sch├╢n entwickelt, aus dem Scho├Яe, dem Mutterleib der Zeit sch├╢n geboren.

Grassmann

Die G├╢tter folgten тАУ des Opfers Werken, umgeben rings es тАУ wie Erd’ der Himmel; Die Wasser n├дhren тАУ mit Lob das Kindlein, das sch├╢ngeborne тАУ im Schooss des Opfers.

Sayana bhashya empty

Wilson

English translation:

тАЬ[5-6] Agni is grateful as nourishment, vast as the earth, productive (of vegetable food), as a mountain, delightful as water; he is like a horse urged to a charge in battle and like flowing waters; who can arrest him?тАЭ

Commentary by S─Бyaс╣Зa: с╣Ъgveda-bh─Бс╣гya

Puс╣гс╣нirna raс╣Зv─Б = like grateful nourishment; kс╣гitirna pс╣Ыthv─л = like the vast earth; the epithets applied to objects also apply to Agni

Jamison Brereton

Like thriving that brings delight, like a broad (place of) peaceful dwelling, like a mountain a source of benefit, like a gush (of water) that brings blessings;

Jamison Brereton Notes

It is tempting to take the adjectives raс╣Зv─Б╠Б, pс╣Ыthv─л╠Б, and ┼Ыambh├║ as referring to Agni and specifying the term of comparison with the item in the simile (тАЬbroad like a place of peaceful dwelling,тАЭ etc.), and in fact Geldner (mostly) and Renou (entirely) give in to this temptation. However, all three adjectives agree in gender with the noun in the simile (fem. in the first two, neut. in the last), and although it would be possible to explain this agreement as тАЬattractionтАЭ (so Renou), a simpler solution is to assume that the adjective belongs with the comparandum. (So also Witzel Got┼Н.) The phrase gir├нr n├б bh├║jma poses a different problem, in that gir├н- is masc., and therefore if bhujm├б is an adjective, it does not modify gir├нс╕е тАФ unlike the otherwise entirely parallel expression in VIII.50.2 (V─Бlakh.) gir├нr n├б bhujm─Б╠Б. We should either emend the text (├а bhujm─Б╠Б, so Grassmann) to agree with that passage (тАЬlike a beneficial mountainтАЭ) or take it as a nominalized neut. тАЬsource of benefit,тАЭ the solution adopted in the published translation On this characteristic of mountains, see, e.g., I.55.3.

Griffith

Like a swan sitting in the floods he pants wisest in mind mid men he wakes at morn.
A Sage like Soma, sprung from Law, he grew like some young creature, mighty, shining far.

рдЕрдиреНрд╡рдп: рдпрд╕реНрддрдореЗрддрдВ рдкрд░рдорд╛рддреНрдорд╛рдирдВ рд░рдгреНрд╡рд╛ рдкреБрд╖реНрдЯрд┐рд░реНрдиреЗрд╡ рдХреНрд╖рд┐рддрд┐рдГ рдкреГрдереНрд╡реА рдиреЗрд╡ рдЧрд┐рд░рд┐рд░реНрднреБрдЬреНрдордиреЗрд╡ рдХреНрд╖реЛрджрдГ рд╢рдВрднреБрдиреЗрд╡рд╛рд╜рдЬреНрдордиреНрдирддреНрдпреЛ рдиреЗрд╡ рд╕рд░реНрдЧрдкреНрд░рддрдХреНрддрдГ рдХреНрд╖реЛрджреЛ рдиреЗрд╡ рдХреЛ рд╡рд░рд╛рддреЗ рд╡реГрдгреБрддреЗ рд╕ рдкреВрд░реНрдгрд╡рд┐рджреНрдпреЛ рднрд╡рддрд┐ рее рей рее

рджрдпрд╛рдирдиреНрдж-рд╕рд░рд╕реНрд╡рддреА (рд╣рд┐) - рд╡рд┐рд╖рдпрдГ

Sayana bhashya empty

Wilson

English translation:

тАЬ[7-8] He is the kind kinsman of the waters, like a brother to his sister; he consumes the forest as a r─Бj─Б (destroys) his enemies; when excited by the wind, he traverses the woods, and shears the hairs of the earth.тАЭ

Commentary by S─Бyaс╣Зa: с╣Ъgveda-bh─Бс╣гya

The fruit, flowers, grasses, shrubs and the like are called roma pс╣Ыthivy─Бс╕е = hairs of the earth

Jamison Brereton

Akin to the rivers, like a brother to his sisters,
(yet,) like a king his vassals, he devours the wood.

Jamison Brereton Notes

├нbhya- is found only here in the RV, though it occurs later. It is a derivative of ├нbha-, which is slightly better attested in the RV and seems to mean тАШ(group of) servants, retinueтАЩ. Tr. of ├нbhy─Бn split dramatically between тАШwealthyтАЩ (Geldner тАЬdie Reichen,тАЭ Witzel Got┼Н тАЬdie Beg├╝tertenтАЭ) and тАШvassal, servantтАЩ (Renou тАЬles vassauxтАЭ), and the P─Бli cognate ibbha- is also glossed with both, though with the тАШvassalтАЩ sense first and dominant (see the newest ed. of the PTS dictionary, 2001 s.v.: тАЬa member of a kingтАЩs entourage; a vassal; dependent; wealthyтАЭ). I will not enter into a reexamination of all the Sanskrit and MIA evidence, but in my opinion context here favors тАШvassal, dependentтАЩ rather than тАШwealthyтАЩ. See ProferesтАЩs disc. of vss. 7-9 (2007: 111-12); although he refuses to tr. ├нbhy─Бn, he argues that it refers тАЬto those who are subordinated to the king,тАЭ quite possibly the clans тАФ as the AV passage he adduces suggests: AV IV.22.7 v├н┼Ыo addhi тАЬeat the clans,тАЭ addressed to the king at his consecration.

рд╕рд╛рдпрдг-рднрд╛рд╖реНрдпрдореН

Sayana bhashya empty

Wilson

English translation:

тАЬ[9-10] He breathes amidst the waters like a sitting swan; awakened at the dawn, he restores by his operations consciousness to men; he is a creator, like Soma; born from the waters (where he lurked) like an animal with coiled-up limbs, he became enlarged and his light (spread) afar.тАЭ

Commentary by S─Бyaс╣Зa: с╣Ъgveda-bh─Бс╣гya

Creator like Soma: soma na vedhaс╕е; just as soma creates or causes ‘plural nts’ to grow, so Agni creates or extracts from them their nutritive faculty. et─Бvad v─Б idam annam ann─Бda┼Ыca soma eva annam agnir ann─Бdaс╕е: inasmuch as there is food and feeder, so soma is the food, and the feeder is agni (V─Бjasneyi Yajuс╣г)

Jamison Brereton

He hisses like a wild goose sitting in the waters;
awakening at dawn, he is the most conspicuous to the clans by his
intention.

Jamison Brereton Notes

Strictly speaking, given the position of n├б, the aps├║ should go with the frame, not the simile: тАЬhe hisses in the waters, like тАжтАЭ This is presumably a reference both to the myth of AgniтАЩs hiding in the waters treated earlier in the hymn and to his identification with Ap─Бm Nap─Бt, but the haс╣Гs├б- is surely sitting in the waters, too - though the primary point of comparison is the hissing noise both make.

How to construe gen. vi┼Ы─Б╠Бm is not clear. I take it loosely as a datival gen. with the c├йtiс╣гс╣нhaс╕е, while Renou considers it dependent on a тАЬvirtualтАЭ vi┼Ыp├бti- (тАЬle plus remarquable тАж de (ceux qui pr├йsident aux) tribusтАЭ), and Geldner and Witzel Got┼Н, in slightly different ways, take it as dependent on uс╣гarbh├║d (Geldner тАЬder Fr├╝hwache der AnsiedelungenтАЭ; Witzel Got┼Н тАЬder von Niederlassungen am Morgen wach wirdтАЭ). ProferesтАЩs rendering is quite awkward, but closest to my own in intent: тАЬthe clans’ brightest as concerns strategems.тАЭ

Griffith

Jamison Brereton

Like soma, a ritual expert, begotten of truth;
ranging widely like livestock with their young, far-radiant.

Jamison Brereton Notes

The simile in cd is unclear; its purport depends on what meaning is assigned to multivalent vibh├║-, whose senses include тАШconspicuousтАЩ, тАШdistinguishedтАЩ, and тАШwide-rangingтАЩ, to which Geldner adds тАШsich mehrendтАЩ, Renou тАШabondantтАЩ, and Witzel Got┼Н тАШkr├дftigтАЩ.

In GeldnerтАЩs and RenouтАЩs readings, the point of the simile would be the size of the herd and its growth through the birth of its young. This is certainly possible, and the point of contact with Agni would be the growth, that is, the blazing up, of the fire after its kindling. However, I do not see тАШincreaseтАЩ as one of the core meanings of v├н тИЪbh┼л and prefer тАШwide-rangingтАЩ here, with the simile expressing the grazing behavior of herds and the frame the fireтАЩs tendency to spread. See 8b v├бn─Б vy ├бsth─Бt тАЬhe has spread out through the wood.тАЭ The final word of 10, d┼лr├йbh─Бс╕е тАШfar-radiantтАЩ, may support this interpretation. I do admit, however, that ┼Ы├н┼Ыv─Б тАШwith their youngтАЩ makes less sense than in the Geldner/Renou interpretation.

Note that the opening of the last hemistich of the hymn, pa┼Ы├║r n├б ┼Ы─л╠Б┼Ыv─Б, тАЬunpacksтАЭ the hymnтАЩs first word pa┼Ыv─Б╠Бin a type of ring comp., and that this phrase was in a sense anticipated by 4b panv─Б╠Бs├║┼Ыi┼Ыvim.

рдкреБрдирдГ рд╕ рдХреАрджреГрд╢ рдЗрддреНрдпреБрдкрджрд┐рд╢реНрдпрддреЗ рее

рдкрджрд╛рд░реНрдерд╛рдиреНрд╡рдпрднрд╛рд╖рд╛рдГ - (рд╢реНрд╡рд╕рд┐рддрд┐) рдпреЛрд╜рдЧреНрдирд┐рдирд╛ рдкреНрд░рд╛рдгрд╛рд╜рдкрд╛рдирдЪреЗрд╖реНрдЯрд╛рдВ рдХрд░реЛрддрд┐ (рдЕрдкреНрд╕реБ) рдЬрд▓реЗрд╖реБ (рд╣рдВрд╕рдГ) рдкрдХреНрд╖рд┐рд╡рд┐рд╢реЗрд╖рдГ (рд╕реАрджрдиреН) рдЧрдЪреНрдЫрдиреНрдирд╛рдЧрдЪреНрдЫрдиреНрдирд┐рдордЬреНрдЬрдиреНрдиреБрдиреНрдордЬреНрдЬрдиреН рд╡рд╛ (рдХреНрд░рддреНрд╡рд╛) рдХреНрд░рддреБрдирд╛ рдкреНрд░рдЬреНрдЮрдпрд╛ рд╕реНрд╡рдХреАрдпреЗрди рдХрд░реНрдордгрд╛ рд╡рд╛ (рдЪреЗрддрд┐рд╖реНрдардГ) рдЕрддрд┐рд╢рдпреЗрди рдЪреЗрддрдпрд┐рддрд╛ (рд╡рд┐рд╢рд╛рдореН) рдкреНрд░рдЬрд╛рдирд╛рдореН (рдЙрд╖рд░реНрднреБрддреН) рдЙрд╖рд╕рд┐ рд╕рд░реНрд╡рд╛рдиреНрдмреЛрдзрдпрддрд┐ рд╕рдГред рдЕрддреНрд░реЛрд╖рд░реБрдкрдкрджрд╛рджреН рдмреБрдзрдзрд╛рддреЛрдГ рдХреНрд╡рд┐рдкреНред рдмрд╢реЛрднрд╖рд┐рддрд┐ рднрддреНрд╡рдВ рдЪред (рд╕реЛрдордГ) рдУрд╖рдзрд┐рд╕рдореВрд╣рдГ (рди) рдЗрд╡ (рд╡реЗрдзрд╛рдГ) рдкреЛрд╖рдХрдГ (рдЛрддрдкреНрд░рдЬрд╛рддрдГ) рдХрд╛рд░рдгрд╛рджреБрддреНрдкрджреНрдп рдЛрддреЗ рд╡рд╛рдпрд╛рд╡реБрджрдХреЗ рдкреНрд░рд╕рд┐рджреНрдзрдГ (рдкрд╢реБрдГ) рдЧрд╡рд╛рджрд┐рдГ (рди) рдЗрд╡ (рд╢рд┐рд╢реНрд╡рд╛) рд╢рд┐рд╢реБрдирд╛ рд╡рддреНрд╕рд╛рджрд┐рдирд╛ (рд╡рд┐рднреБрдГ) рд╡реНрдпрд╛рдкрдХрдГ (рджреВрд░реЗрднрд╛рдГ) рджреВрд░рджреЗрд╢реЗ рднрд╛ рджреАрдкреНрддрдпреЛ рдпрд╕реНрдп рд╕рдГ рее рел рее

рджрдпрд╛рдирдиреНрдж-рд╕рд░рд╕реНрд╡рддреА (рд╣рд┐) - рднрд╛рд╡рд╛рд░реНрдердГ

рднрд╛рд╡рд╛рд░реНрдерднрд╛рд╖рд╛рдГ - рдЕрддреНрд░реЛрдкрдорд╛рд▓рдЩреНрдХрд╛рд░рдГред рдпрдерд╛ рд╡рд┐рджреНрдпреБрддрд╛рдЧреНрдирд┐рдирд╛ рд╡рд┐рдирд╛ рдХрд╕реНрдпрдЪрд┐рддреНрдкреНрд░рд╛рдгрд┐рдиреЛ рд╡реНрдпрд╡рд╣рд╛рд░рд╕рд┐рджреНрдзрд┐рд░реНрднрд╡рд┐рддреБрдВ рди рд╢рдХреНрдпрд╛рд╕реНрддрд┐ред рддрд╕реНрдорд╛рджрдпрдВ рд╡рд┐рджреНрдпрдпрд╛ рд╕рдореНрдкрд░реАрдХреНрд╖реНрдп рдХрд╛рд░реНрдпреНрдпреЗрд╖реБ рд╕рдВрдпреЛрдЬрд┐рддрдГ рдмрд╣реВрдирд┐ рд╕реБрдЦрд╛рдирд┐ рд╕рд╛рдзреНрдиреЛрддреАрддрд┐ рее рел рее рдЕрддреНрд░реЗрд╢реНрд╡рд░рд╡рд┐рджреНрдпреБрджрдЧреНрдирд┐рдЧреБрдгрд╡рд░реНрдгрдирд╛рджреЗрддрджрд░реНрдерд╕реНрдп рдкреВрд░реНрд╡рд╕реВрдХреНрддрд╛рд░реНрдереЗрди рд╕рд╣ рд╕рдЩреНрдЧрддрд┐рд░рд╕реНрддреАрддрд┐ рдмреЛрджреНрдзрд╡реНрдпрдореН рее
